href="/wiki/File:Wikibooks-logo-en-noslogan.svg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/df/Wikibooks-logo-en-noslogan.svg/16px-Wikibooks-logo-en-noslogan.svg.png" width="16" height="16" class="noviewer" //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/df/Wikibooks-logo-en-noslogan.svg/32px-Wikibooks-logo-en-noslogan.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="400" data-file-height="400" /></a> <a href="https://en.wikibooks.org/wiki/Ruby_Programming" class="extiw" title="wikibooks:Ruby Programming">Ruby Programming</a> at Wikibooks</li>/n</ul>/n</td>/n</tr>/n</table>/n<p><b>Ruby</b> is a <a href="/wiki/Dynamic_programming_language" title="Dynamic programming language">dynamic</a>, <a href="/wiki/Reflection_(computer_science)" class="mw-redirect" title="Reflection (computer science)">reflective</a>, <a href="/wiki/Object-oriented_programming_language" class="mw-redirect" title="Object-oriented programming language">object-oriented</a>, <a href="/wiki/General-purpose_programming_language" title="General-purpose programming language">general-purpose programming language</a>. It was designed and developed in the mid-1990s by <a href="/wiki/Yukihiro_Matsumoto" title="Yukihiro Matsumoto">Yukihiro "Matz" Matsumoto</a> in <a href="/wiki/Japan" title="Japan">Japan</a>.</p>/n<p>According to its creator, Ruby was influenced by <a href="/wiki/Perl" title="Perl">Perl</a>, <a href="/wiki/Smalltalk" title="Smalltalk">Smalltalk</a>, <a href="/wiki/Eiffel_(programming_language)" title="Eiffel (programming language)">Eiffel</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ada_(programming_language)" title="Ada (programming language)">Ada</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Lisp_(programming_language)" title="Lisp (programming language)">Lisp</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-11" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-11">[11]</a></sup> It supports multiple <a href="/wiki/Programming_paradigm" title="Programming paradigm">programming paradigms</a>, including <a href="/wiki/Functional_programming" title="Functional programming">functional</a>, <a href="/wiki/Object-oriented_programming" title="Object-oriented programming">object-oriented</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Imperative_programming" title="Imperative programming">imperative</a>. It also has a <a href="/wiki/Dynamic_type" class="mw-redirect" title="Dynamic type">dynamic type</a> system and automatic <a href="/wiki/Memory_management" title="Memory management">memory management</a>.</p>/n<p></p>/n<div id="toc" class="toc">/n<div class="toctitle">/n<h2>Contents</h2>/n</div>/n<ul>/n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-1"><a href="#History"><span classes</span></a></li>/n</ul>/n</li>/n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-26"><a href="#Exceptions"><span class="tocnumber">8.6</span> <span class="toctext">Exceptions</span></a></li>/n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-27"><a href="#Metaprogramming"><span class="tocnumber">8.7</span> <span class="toctext">Metaprogramming</span></a></li>/n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-28"><a href="#More_examples"><span class="tocnumber">8.8</span> <span class="toctext">More examples</span></a></li>/n</ul>/n</li>/n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-29"><a href="#Implementations"><span class="tocnumber">9</span> <span class="toctext">Implementations</span></a>/n<ul>/n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-30"><a href="#Matz.27s_Ruby_interpreter"><span class="tocnumber">9.1</span> <span class="toctext">Matz's Ruby interpreter</span></a></li>/n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-31"><a href="#Alternate_implementations"><span class="tocnumber">9.2</span> <span class="toctext">Alternate implementations</span></a></li>/n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-32"><a href="#Platform_support"><span class="tocnumber">9.3</span> <span class="toctext">Platform support</span></a></li>/n</ul>/n</li>/n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-33"><a href="#Repositories_and_libraries"><span class="tocnumber">10</span> <span class="toctext">Repositories and libraries</span></a></li>/n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-34"><a href="#See_also"><span class="tocnumber">11</span> <span class="toctext">See also</span></a></li>/n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-35"><a href="#References"><span class="tocnumber">12</span> <span class="toctext">References</span></a></li>/n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-36"><a href="#Further_reading"><span class="tocnumber">13</span> <span class="toctext">Further reading</span></a></li>/n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-37"><a href="#External_links"><span class="tocnumber">14</span> <span class="toctext">External links</span></a></li>/n</ul>/n</div>/n<p></p>/n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="History">History</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Ruby_(programming_language)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: History">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>/n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Early_concept">Early concept</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Ruby_(programming_language)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Early concept">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>/n<p>Ruby was conceived on February 24, 1993. In a 1999 post to the <i>ruby-talk</i> mailing list, Ruby author Yukihiro Matsumoto describes some of his early ideas about the language:<sup id="cite_ref-12" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-12">[12]</a></sup></p>/n<blockquote class="templatequote">/n<p>I was talking with my colleague about the possibility of an object-oriented scripting language. I knew Perl (Perl4, not Perl5), but I didn't like it really, because it had the smell of a <a href="/wiki/Toy_language" class="mw-redirect" title="Toy language">toy language</a> (it still has). The object-oriented language seemed very promising. I knew <a href="/wiki/Python_(programming_language)" title="Python (programming language)">Python</a> then. But I didn't like it, because I didn't think it was a true object-oriented language&#160;— OO features appeared to be add-on to the language. As a language maniac and OO fan for 15 years, I really wanted a genuine object-oriented, easy-to-use scripting language. I looked for but couldn't find one. So I decided to make it.</p>/n</blockquote>/n<p>Matsumoto describes the design of Ruby as being like a simple <a href="/wiki/Lisp_(programming_language)" title="Lisp (programming language)">Lisp</a> language at its core, with an object system like that of Smalltalk, blocks inspired by <a href="/wiki/Higher-order_function" title="Higher-order function">higher-order functions</a>, and practical utility like that of Perl.<sup id="cite_ref-13" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-13">[13]</a></sup></p>/n<h3><span id="The_name_&quot;Ruby&quot;"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="The_name_.22Ruby.22">The name "Ruby"</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Ruby_(programming_language)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: The name &quot;Ruby&quot;">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>/n<p>The name "Ruby" originated during an online chat session between Matsumoto and Keiju Ishitsuka on February 24, 1993, before any code had been written for the language.<sup id="cite_ref-rubyconf-history-of-ruby_14-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-rubyconf-history-of-ruby-14">[14]</a></sup> Initially two names were proposed: "<a href="/wiki/Coral" title="Coral">Coral</a>" and "<a href="/wiki/Ruby" title="Ruby">Ruby</a>". Matsumoto chose the latter in a later e-mail to Ishitsuka.<sup id="cite_ref-15" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-15">[15]</a></sup> Matsumoto later noted a factor in choosing the name "Ruby"&#160;– it was the <a href="/wiki/Birthstone" title="Birthstone">birthstone</a> of one of his colleagues.<sup id="cite_ref-16" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-16">[16]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-17" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-17">[17]</a></sup></p>/n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="First_publication">First publication</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Ruby_(programming_language)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: First publication">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>/n<p>The first public release of Ruby 0.95 was announced on Japanese domestic <a href="/wiki/Newsgroup" class="mw-redirect" title="Newsgroup">newsgroups</a> on December 21, 1995.<sup id="cite_ref-18" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-18">[18]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-19" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-19">[19]</a></sup> Subsequently, three more versions of Ruby were released in two days.<sup id="cite_ref-rubyconf-history-of-ruby_14-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-rubyconf-history-of-ruby-14">[14]</a></sup> The release coincided with the launch of the <a href="/wiki/Japanese_language" title="Japanese language">Japanese-language</a> <i>ruby-list</i> mailing list, which was the first mailing list for the new language.</p>/n<p>Already present at this stage of development were many of the features familiar in later releases of Ruby, including <a href="/wiki/Object-oriented_programming" title="Object-oriented programming">object-oriented</a> design, <a href="/wiki/Class_(computer_science)" class="mw-redirect" title="Class (computer science)">classes</a> with inheritance, <a href="/wiki/Mixin" title="Mixin">mixins</a>, <a href="/wiki/Iterator" title="Iterator">iterators</a>, <a href="/wiki/Closure_(computer_science)" class="mw-redirect" title="Closure (computer science)">closures</a>, <a href="/wiki/Exception_handling" title="Exception handling">exception handling</a> and <a href="/wiki/Garbage_collection_(computer_science)" title="Garbage collection (computer science)">garbage collection</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-20" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-20">[20]</a></sup></p>/n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Early_releases">Early releases</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Ruby_(programming_language)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Early releases">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>/n<p>Following the release of Ruby 0.95 in 1995, several stable versions of Ruby were released in the following years:</p>/n<ul>/n<li>Ruby 1.0: December 25, 1996<sup id="cite_ref-rubyconf-history-of-ruby_14-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-rubyconf-history-of-ruby-14">[14]</a></sup></li>/n<li>Ruby 1.2: December 1998</li>/n<li>Ruby 1.4: August 1999</li>/n<li>Ruby 1.6: September 2000</li>/n</ul>/n<p>In 1997, the first article about Ruby was published on the Web. In the same year, Matsumoto was hired by netlab.jp to work on Ruby as a full-time developer.<sup id="cite_ref-rubyconf-history-of-ruby_14-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-rubyconf-history-of-ruby-14">[14]</a></sup></p>/n<p>In 1998, the Ruby Application Archive was launched by Matsumoto, along with a simple English-language homepage for Ruby.<sup id="cite_ref-rubyconf-history-of-ruby_14-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-rubyconf-history-of-ruby-14">[14]</a></sup></p>/n<p>In 1999, the first English language mailing list <i>ruby-talk</i> began, which signaled a growing interest in the language outside Japan.<sup id="cite_ref-linuxdevcenter_21-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-linuxdevcenter-21">[21]</a></sup> In this same year, Matsumoto and Keiju Ishitsuka wrote the first book on Ruby, <i>The Object-oriented Scripting Language Ruby</i> (オブジェクト指向スクリプト言語 Ruby), which was published in Japan in October 1999. It would be followed in the early 2000s by around 20 books on Ruby published in Japanese.<sup id="cite_ref-rubyconf-history-of-ruby_14-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-rubyconf-history-of-ruby-14">[14]</a></sup></p>/n<p>By 2000, Ruby was more popular than Python in Japan.<sup id="cite_ref-22" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-22">[22]</a></sup> In September 2000, the first English language book <i><a href="/wiki/Programming_Ruby" title="Programming Ruby">Programming Ruby</a></i> was printed, which was later freely released to the public, further widening the adoption of Ruby amongst English speakers. In early 2002, the English-language <i>ruby-talk</i> mailing list was receiving more messages than the Japanese-language <i>ruby-list</i>, demonstrating Ruby's increasing popularity in the English-speaking world.</p>/n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Ruby_1.8">Ruby 1.8</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Ruby_(programming_language)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Ruby 1.8">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>/n<p>Ruby 1.8 was initially released in August 2003, was stable for a long time, and was retired June 2013.<sup id="cite_ref-1.8.7-retirement_23-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-1.8.7-retirement-23">[23]</a></sup> Although deprecated, there is still code based on it. Ruby 1.8 is only partially compatible with Ruby 1.9.</p>/n<p>Ruby 1.8 has been the subject of several industry standards. The language specifications for Ruby were developed by the Open Standards Promotion Center of the Information-Technology Promotion Agency (a <a href="/wiki/Government_of_Japan" title="Government of Japan">Japanese government</a> agency) for submission to the <a href="/wiki/Japanese_Industrial_Standards_Committee" title="Japanese Industrial Standards Committee">Japanese Industrial Standards Committee</a> (JISC) and then to the <a href="/wiki/International_Organization_for_Standardization" title="International Organization for Standardization">International Organization for Standardization</a> (ISO). It was accepted as a Japanese Industrial Standard (JIS X 3017) in 2011<sup id="cite_ref-24" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-24">[24]</a></sup> and an international standard (<a href="/w/index.php?title=ISO/IEC_30170&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="ISO/IEC 30170 (page does not exist)">ISO/IEC 30170</a>) in 2012.<sup id="cite_ref-25" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-25">[25]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-26" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-26">[26]</a></sup></p>/n<p>Around 2005, interest in the Ruby language surged in tandem with <a href="/wiki/Ruby_on_Rails" title="Ruby on Rails">Ruby on Rails</a>, a <a href="/wiki/Web_framework" title="Web framework">web framework</a> written in Ruby. Rails is frequently credited with increasing awareness of Ruby.<sup id="cite_ref-27" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-27">[27]</a></sup></p>/n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Ruby_1.9">Ruby 1.9</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Ruby_(programming_language)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Ruby 1.9">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>/n<p>Ruby 1.9 was released in December 2007. Effective with Ruby 1.9.3, released October 31, 2011,<sup id="cite_ref-28" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-28">[28]</a></sup> Ruby switched from being dual-licensed under the Ruby License and the GPL to being dual-licensed under the Ruby License and the two-clause BSD license.<sup id="cite_ref-29" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-29">[29]</a></sup> Adoption of 1.9 was slowed by changes from 1.8 that required many popular third party <a href="#Repositories_and_libraries">gems</a> to be rewritten.</p>/n<p>Ruby 1.9 introduces many significant changes over the 1.8 series.<sup id="cite_ref-30" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-30">[30]</a></sup> Examples:</p>/n<ul>/n<li><a href="/wiki/Local_variable" title="Local variable">block local</a> variables (variables that are local to the <a href="/wiki/Block_(programming)" title="Block (programming)">block</a> in which they are declared)</li>/n<li>an additional <a href="/wiki/Anonymous_function" title="Anonymous function">lambda</a> syntax: <code class="mw-highlight" id="" style="" dir="ltr"><span class="n">f</span> <span class="o">=</span> <span class="o">-&gt;</span><span class="p">(</span><span class="n">a</span><span class="p">,</span><span class="n">b</span><span class="p">)</span> <span class="p">{</span> <span class="nb">puts</span> <span class="n">a</span> <span class="o">+</span> <span class="n">b</span> <span class="p">}</span></code></li>/n<li>per-string <a href="/wiki/Character_encoding" title="Character encoding">character encodings</a> are supported</li>/n<li>new socket API (<a href="/wiki/IPv6" title="IPv6">IPv6</a> support)</li>/n<li><code>require_relative</code> import security</li>/n</ul>/n<p>Ruby 1.9 has been obsolete since February 23, 2015,<sup id="cite_ref-31" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-31">[31]</a></sup> and it will no longer receive bug and security fixes. Users are advised to upgrade to a more recent version.</p>/n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Ruby_2.0">Ruby 2.0</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Ruby_(programming_language)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Ruby 2.0">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>/n<p>Ruby 2.0 added several new features, including:</p>/n<ul>/n<li>method keyword arguments,</li>/n<li>a new method, <code>Module#prepend</code>, for extending a class,</li>/n<li>a new literal for creating an array of symbols,</li>/n<li>new API for the <a href="/wiki/Lazy_evaluation" title="Lazy evaluation">lazy evaluation</a> of Enumerables, and</li>/n<li>a new convention of using #to_h to convert objects to Hashes.<sup id="cite_ref-32" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-32">[32]</a></sup></li>/n</ul>/n<p>Ruby 2.0 is intended to be fully backward compatible with Ruby 1.9.3. As of the official 2.0.0 release on February 24, 2013, there were only five known (minor) incompatibilities.<sup id="cite_ref-2-0-release-incompatibilities_33-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-2-0-release-incompatibilities-33">[33]</a></sup></p>/n<p>It has been obsolete since February 22, 2016,<sup id="cite_ref-34" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-34">[34]</a></sup> and it will no longer receive bug and security fixes. Users are advised to upgrade to a more recent version.</p>/n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Ruby_2.1">Ruby 2.1</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Ruby_(programming_language)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Ruby 2.1">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>/n<p>Ruby 2.1.0 was released on Christmas Day in 2013.<sup id="cite_ref-2-1-0-release_35-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-2-1-0-release-35">[35]</a></sup> The release includes speed-ups, bugfixes, and library updates.</p>/n<p>Starting with 2.1.0, Ruby's versioning policy is more like <a href="/wiki/Semantic_versioning" class="mw-redirect" title="Semantic versioning">semantic versioning</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-semantic-versioning_36-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-semantic-versioning-36">[36]</a></sup> Although similar, Ruby's versioning policy is not compatible with semantic versioning:</p>/n<table class="wikitable">/n<tr>/n<th>Ruby</th>/n<th>Semantic versioning</th>/n</tr>/n<tr>/n<td><b>MAJOR</b>: Increased when incompatible change which can’t be released in MINOR. Reserved for special events.</td>/n<td><b>MAJOR</b>: Increased when you make incompatible API changes.</td>/n</tr>/n<tr>/n<td><b>MINOR</b>: increased every Christmas, <i>may be</i> API incompatible.</td>/n<td><b>MINOR</b>: increased when you add functionality in a <i>backwards-compatible</i> manner.</td>/n</tr>/n<tr>/n<td><b>TEENY</b>: security or bug fix which maintains API compatibility. May be increased more than 10 (such as 2.1.11), and will be released every 2–3 months.</td>/n<td><b>PATCH</b>: increased when you make backwards-compatible bug fixes.</td>/n</tr>/n<tr>/n<td><b>PATCH</b>: number of commits since last MINOR release (will be reset at 0 when releasing MINOR).</td>/n<td>-</td>/n</tr>/n</table>/n<p>Semantic versioning also provides additional labels for pre-release and build metadata are available as extensions to the MAJOR.MINOR.PATCH format, not available at Ruby.</p>/n<p>Ruby 2.1 has been obsolete since April 1, 2017,<sup id="cite_ref-37" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-37">[37]</a></sup> and it will no longer receive bug and security fixes. Users are advised to upgrade to a more recent version.</p>/n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Ruby_2.2">Ruby 2.2</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Ruby_(programming_language)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Ruby 2.2">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>/n<p>Ruby 2.2.0 was released on Christmas Day in 2014.<sup id="cite_ref-2-2-0-release_38-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-2-2-0-release-38">[38]</a></sup> The release includes speed-ups, bugfixes, and library updates and removes some deprecated APIs. Most notably, Ruby 2.2.0 introduces changes to memory handling&#160;–  an incremental garbage collector, support for garbage collection of symbols and the option to compile directly against jemalloc. It also contains experimental support for using <a href="/wiki/Vfork" class="mw-redirect" title="Vfork">vfork</a>(2) with system() and spawn(), and added support for the <a href="/wiki/Unicode" title="Unicode">Unicode</a> 7.0 specification.</p>/n<p>Features that were made obsolete or removed include callcc, the DL library, Digest::HMAC, lib/rational.rb, lib/complex.rb, GServer, Logger::Application as well as various C API functions.<sup id="cite_ref-obsolete-or-gone-in-2.2_39-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-obsolete-or-gone-in-2.2-39">[39]</a></sup></p>/n<dl>/n<dt>PowerPC64 performance</dt>/n<dd>Since version 2.2.1,<sup id="cite_ref-40" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-40">[40]</a></sup> Ruby MRI performance on <a href="/wiki/Ppc64" title="Ppc64">PowerPC64</a> was improved.<sup id="cite_ref-41" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-41">[41]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-42" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-42">[42]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-43" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-43">[43]</a></sup></dd>/n</dl>/n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Ruby_2.3">Ruby 2.3</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Ruby_(programming_language)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Ruby 2.3">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>/n<p>Ruby 2.3.0 was released on Christmas Day in 2015. A few notable changes include:</p>/n<ul>/n<li>The ability to mark all string literals as frozen by default with a consequently large performance increase in string operations.<sup id="cite_ref-44" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-44">[44]</a></sup></li>/n<li>Hash comparison to allow direct checking of key/value pairs instead of just keys.</li>/n<li>A new <a href="/wiki/Safe_navigation_operator" title="Safe navigation operator">safe navigation operator</a> <code>&amp;.</code> that can ease nil handling (e.g. instead of <code class="mw-highlight" id="" style="" dir="ltr"><span class="k">if</span> <span class="n">obj</span> <span class="o">&amp;&amp;</span> <span class="n">obj</span><span class="o">.</span><span class="n">foo</span> <span class="o">&amp;&amp;</span> <span class="n">obj</span><span class="o">.</span><span class="n">foo</span><span class="o">.</span><span class="n">bar</span></code>, we can use <code>if obj&amp;.foo&amp;.bar</code>).</li>/n<li>The <i>did_you_mean</i> gem is now bundled by default and required on startup to automatically suggest similar name matches on a <i>NameError</i> or <i>NoMethodError</i>.</li>/n<li><i>Hash#dig</i> and <i>Array#dig</i> to easily extract deeply nested values (e.g. given <code class="mw-highlight" id="" style="" dir="ltr"><span class="n">profile</span> <span class="o">=</span> <span class="p">{</span> <span class="ss">social</span><span class="p">:</span> <span class="p">{</span> <span class="ss">wikipedia</span><span class="p">:</span> <span class="p">{</span> <span class="nb">name</span><span class="p">:</span> <span class="s1">'Foo Baz'</span> <span class="p">}</span> <span class="p">}</span> <span class="p">}</span></code>, the value <i>Foo Baz</i> can now be retrieved by <code>profile.dig(:social,&#160;:wikipedia,&#160;:name)</code>).</li>/n<li><code>.grep_v(regexp)</code> which will match all negative examples of a given regular expression in addition to other new features.</li>/n</ul>/n<p>The 2.3 branch also includes many performance improvements, updates, and bugfixes including changes to Proc#call, Socket and IO use of exception keywords, Thread#name handling, default passive Net::FTP connections, and Rake being removed from stdlib.<sup id="cite_ref-Ruby_2.3.0_NEWS_45-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ruby_2.3.0_NEWS-45">[45]</a></sup></p>/n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Ruby_2.4">Ruby 2.4</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Ruby_(programming_language)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Ruby 2.4">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>/n<p>Ruby 2.4.0 was released on Christmas Day in 2016. A few notable changes include:</p>/n<ul>/n<li>Binding#irb: Start a REPL session similar to binding.pry</li>/n<li>Unify <i>Fixnum</i> and <i>Bignum</i> into <i>Integer</i> class</li>/n<li>String supports Unicode case mappings, not just ASCII</li>/n<li>a new method, Regexp#match?, which is a faster boolean version of Regexp#match</li>/n<li>Thread deadlock detection now shows threads with their backtrace and dependency</li>/n</ul>/n<p>The 2.4 branch also includes performance improvements to hash table, Array#max, Array#min, and instance variable access.<sup id="cite_ref-:0_46-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:0-46">[46]</a></sup></p>/n<p>Ruby's popularity has been declining since 2014<sup id="cite_ref-47" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-47">[47]</a></sup> , a slip that may be permanent in the face of increasing competition.<sup id="cite_ref-48" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-48">[48]</a></sup></p>/n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Table_of_versions">Table of versions</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Ruby_(programming_language)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Table of versions">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>/n<table class="wikitable">/n<tr>/n<th>Version</th>/n<th>Latest teeny version</th>/n<th>Initial release date</th>/n<th>End of support phase</th>/n<th>End of security maintenance phase</th>/n</tr>/n<tr>/n<td style="background-color: #FDB3AB;" title="Old version, no longer supported" data-sort-value="1.8"><span style="display: none;">Old version, no longer supported:</span> 1.8</td>/n<td>1.8.7-p375<sup padding-left: 0.3em; white-space: nowrap;" title="A future release">Future release</span></div>/n<div style="clear: left;"></div>/n</div>/n</td>/n</tr>/n</table>/n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Philosophy">Philosophy</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Ruby_(programming_language)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Philosophy">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>/n<div class="thumb tright">/n<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Yukihiro_Matsumoto.JPG" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/76/Yukihiro_Matsumoto.JPG/220px-Yukihiro_Matsumoto.JPG" width="220" height="328" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/7/76/Yukihiro_Matsumoto.JPG 1.5x" data-file-width="319" data-file-height="476" /></a>/n<div class="thumbcaption">/n<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Yukihiro_Matsumoto.JPG" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>/n<a href="/wiki/Yukihiro_Matsumoto" title="Yukihiro Matsumoto">Yukihiro Matsumoto</a>, the creator of Ruby</div>/n</div>/n</div>/n<p>Matsumoto has said that Ruby is designed for programmer productivity and fun, following the principles of good <a href="/wiki/User_interface" title="User interface">user interface</a> design.<sup id="cite_ref-76" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-76">[76]</a></sup> At a Google Tech Talk in 2008 Matsumoto further stated, "I hope to see Ruby help every programmer in the world to be productive, and to enjoy programming, and to be happy. That is the primary purpose of Ruby language."<sup id="cite_ref-googletechtalk_77-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-googletechtalk-77">[77]</a></sup> He stresses that systems design needs to emphasize human, rather than computer, needs:<sup id="cite_ref-artima_78-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-artima-78">[78]</a></sup></p>/n<blockquote class="templatequote">/n<p>Often people, especially computer engineers, focus on the machines. They think, "By doing this, the machine will run fast. By doing this, the machine will run more effectively. By doing this, the machine will something something something." They are focusing on machines. But in fact we need to focus on humans, on how humans care about doing programming or operating the application of the machines. We are the masters. They are the slaves.</p>/n</blockquote>/n<p>Ruby is said to follow the <a href="/wiki/Principle_of_least_astonishment" title="Principle of least astonishment">principle of least astonishment</a> (POLA), meaning that the language should behave in such a way as to minimize confusion for experienced users. Matsumoto has said his primary design goal was to make a language that he himself enjoyed using, by minimizing programmer work and possible confusion. He has said that he had not applied the principle of least astonishment to the design of Ruby,<sup id="cite_ref-artima_78-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-artima-78">[78]</a></sup> but nevertheless the phrase has come to be closely associated with the Ruby programming language. The phrase has itself been a source of surprise, as novice users may take it to mean that Ruby's behaviors try to closely match behaviors familiar from other languages. In a May 2005 discussion on the newsgroup comp.lang.ruby, Matsumoto attempted to distance Ruby from POLA, explaining that because any design choice will be surprising to someone, he uses a personal standard in evaluating surprise. If that personal standard remains consistent, there would be few surprises for those familiar with the standard.<sup id="cite_ref-79" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-79">[79]</a></sup></p>/n<p>Matsumoto defined it this way in an interview:<sup id="cite_ref-artima_78-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-artima-78">[78]</a></sup></p>/n<blockquote class="templatequote">/n<p>Everyone has an individual background. Someone may come from <a href="/wiki/Python_(programming_language)" title="Python (programming language)">Python</a>, someone else may come from <a href="/wiki/Perl" title="Perl">Perl</a>, and they may be surprised by different aspects of the language. Then they come up to me and say, 'I was surprised by this feature of the language, so Ruby violates the principle of least surprise.' Wait. Wait. The principle of least surprise is not for you only. The principle of least surprise means principle of least <i>my</i> surprise. And it means the principle of least surprise after you learn Ruby very well. For example, I was a <a href="/wiki/C%2B%2B" title="C++">C++</a> programmer before I started designing Ruby. I programmed in <a href="/wiki/C%2B%2B" title="C++">C++</a> exclusively for two or three years. And after two years of C++ programming, it still surprises me.</p>/n</blockquote>/n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Features">Features</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Ruby_(programming_language)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: Features">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>/n<ul>/n<li>Thoroughly <a href="/wiki/Object-oriented" class="mw-redirect" title="Object-oriented">object-oriented</a> with <a href="/wiki/Inheritance_(computer_science)" class="mw-redirect" title="Inheritance (computer science)">inheritance</a>, <a href="/wiki/Mixin" title="Mixin">mixins</a> and <a href="/wiki/Metaclass" title="Metaclass">metaclasses</a><sup id="cite_ref-stewart_80-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-stewart-80">[80]</a></sup></li>/n<li><a href="/wiki/Dynamic_typing" class="mw-redirect" title="Dynamic typing">Dynamic typing</a> and <a href="/wiki/Duck_typing" title="Duck typing">duck typing</a></li>/n<li>Everything is an <a href="/wiki/Expression_(programming)" class="mw-redirect" title="Expression (programming)">expression</a> (even <a href="/wiki/Statement_(programming)" class="mw-redirect" title="Statement (programming)">statements</a>) and everything is executed <a href="/wiki/Imperative_programming" title="Imperative programming">imperatively</a> (even <a href="/wiki/Declaration_(computer_science)" class="mw-redirect" title="Declaration (computer science)">declarations</a>)</li>/n<li>Succinct and flexible syntax<sup id="cite_ref-venners-productivity_81-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-venners-productivity-81">[81]</a></sup> that minimizes <a href="/wiki/Syntactic_noise" title="Syntactic noise">syntactic noise</a> and serves as a foundation for <a href="/wiki/Domain-specific_languages" class="mw-redirect" title="Domain-specific languages">domain-specific languages</a><sup id="cite_ref-fowler-dsl_82-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-fowler-dsl-82">[82]</a></sup></li>/n<li>Dynamic <a href="/wiki/Reflection_(computer_science)" class="mw-redirect" title="Reflection (computer science)">reflection</a> and <a href="/wiki/Dynamic_programming_language#Object_runtime_alteration" title="Dynamic programming language">alteration</a> of objects to facilitate <a href="/wiki/Metaprogramming" title="Metaprogramming">metaprogramming</a><sup id="cite_ref-codeproject-dynamic_83-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-codeproject-dynamic-83">[83]</a></sup></li>/n<li><a href="/wiki/Closure_(computer_science)" class="mw-redirect" title="Closure (computer science)">Lexical closures</a>, <a href="/wiki/Iterator" title="Iterator">iterators</a> and <a href="/wiki/Generator_(computer_science)" class="mw-redirect" title="Generator (computer science)">generators</a>, with a <a href="#Blocks_and_iterators">block syntax</a><sup id="cite_ref-venners-closures_84-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-venners-closures-84">[84]</a></sup></li>/n<li>Literal notation for <a href="/wiki/Dynamic_array" title="Dynamic array">arrays</a>, <a href="/wiki/Associative_array" title="Associative array">hashes</a>, <a href="/wiki/Regular_expression" title="Regular expression">regular expressions</a> and <a href="/wiki/Symbol_(Lisp)" class="mw-redirect" title="Symbol (Lisp)">symbols</a></li>/n<li>Embedding code in strings (<a href="/wiki/Variable_interpolation" class="mw-redirect" title="Variable interpolation">interpolation</a>)</li>/n<li><a href="/wiki/Default_argument" title="Default argument">Default arguments</a></li>/n<li>Four levels of variable scope (<a href="/wiki/Global_variable" title="Global variable">global</a>, <a href="/wiki/Class_variable" title="Class variable">class</a>, <a href="/wiki/Instance_variable" title="Instance variable">instance</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Local_variable" title="Local variable">local</a>) denoted by <a href="/wiki/Sigil_(computer_programming)" title="Sigil (computer programming)">sigils</a> or the lack thereof</li>/n<li><a href="/wiki/Garbage_collection_(computer_science)" title="Garbage collection (computer science)">Garbage collection</a></li>/n<li><a href="/wiki/First-class_continuation" class="mw-redirect" title="First-class continuation">First-class continuations</a></li>/n<li>Strict boolean <a href="/wiki/Implicit_type_conversion" class="mw-redirect" title="Implicit type conversion">coercion</a> rules (everything is <i>true</i> except <code>false</code> and <code><a href="/wiki/Null_pointer" title="Null pointer">nil</a></code>)</li>/n<li><a href="/wiki/Exception_handling" title="Exception handling">Exception handling</a></li>/n<li><a href="/wiki/Operator_overloading" title="Operator overloading">Operator overloading</a></li>/n<li>Built-in support for <a href="/wiki/Rational_number" title="Rational number">rational numbers</a>, <a href="/wiki/Complex_number" title="Complex number">complex numbers</a> and <a href="/wiki/Arbitrary-precision_arithmetic" title="Arbitrary-precision arithmetic">arbitrary-precision arithmetic</a></li>/n<li>Custom dispatch behavior (through <code>method_missing</code> and <code>const_missing</code>)</li>/n<li>Native <a href="/wiki/Thread_(computer_science)" class="mw-redirect" title="Thread (computer science)">threads</a> and cooperative <a href="/wiki/Fiber_(computer_science)" title="Fiber (computer science)">fibers</a> (fibers are a 1.9/<a href="/wiki/YARV" title="YARV">YARV</a> feature)</li>/n<li>Initial support for <a href="/wiki/Unicode" title="Unicode">Unicode</a> and multiple <a href="/wiki/Character_encoding" title="Character encoding">character encodings</a> (no <a href="/wiki/International_Components_for_Unicode" title="International Components for Unicode">ICU</a> support)<sup id="cite_ref-rubyunicodesupport_85-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-rubyunicodesupport-85">[85]</a></sup></li>/n<li>Native <a href="/wiki/Plug-in_(computing)" title="Plug-in (computing)">plug-in</a> API in <a href="/wiki/C_(programming_language)" title="C (programming language)">C</a></li>/n<li><a href="/wiki/Interactive_Ruby_Shell" title="Interactive Ruby Shell">Interactive Ruby Shell</a> (a <a href="/wiki/Read%E2%80%93eval%E2%80%93print_loop" title="Read–eval–print loop">REPL</a>)</li>/n<li>Centralized package management through <a href="/wiki/RubyGems" title="RubyGems">RubyGems</a></li>/n<li>Implemented on all major platforms</li>/n<li>Large standard library, including modules for <a href="/wiki/YAML" title="YAML">YAML</a>, <a href="/wiki/JSON" title="JSON">JSON</a>, <a href="/wiki/XML" title="XML">XML</a>, <a href="/wiki/Common_Gateway_Interface" title="Common Gateway Interface">CGI</a>, <a href="/wiki/OpenSSL" title="OpenSSL">OpenSSL</a>, <a href="/wiki/HTTP" class="mw-redirect" title="HTTP">HTTP</a>, <a href="/wiki/FTP" class="mw-redirect" title="FTP">FTP</a>, <a href="/wiki/RSS" title="RSS">RSS</a>, <a href="/wiki/Curses_(programming_library)" title="Curses (programming library)">curses</a>, <a href="/wiki/Zlib" title="Zlib">zlib</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Tk" class="mw-redirect" title="Tk">Tk</a><sup id="cite_ref-86" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-86">[86]</a></sup></li>/n</ul>/n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Semantics">Semantics</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Ruby_(programming_language)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: Semantics">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>/n<p>Ruby is <a href="/wiki/Object-oriented" class="mw-redirect" title="Object-oriented">object-oriented</a>: every value is an object, including classes and instances of types that many other languages designate as primitives (such as <a href="/wiki/Integer_(computer_science)" title="Integer (computer science)">integers</a>, booleans, and "<a href="/wiki/Null_pointer" title="Null pointer">null</a>"). Variables always hold references to objects. Every <a href="/wiki/Function_(programming)" class="mw-redirect" title="Function (programming)">function</a> is a <a href="/wiki/Method_(programming)" class="mw-redirect" title="Method (programming)">method</a> and methods are always called on an object. Methods defined at the top level scope become methods of the Object class. Since this class is an ancestor of every other class, such methods can be called on any object. They are also visible in all scopes, effectively serving as "global" procedures. Ruby supports <a href="/wiki/Inheritance_(object-oriented_programming)" title="Inheritance (object-oriented programming)">inheritance</a> with <a href="/wiki/Dynamic_dispatch" title="Dynamic dispatch">dynamic dispatch</a>, <a href="/wiki/Mixin" title="Mixin">mixins</a> and singleton methods (belonging to, and defined for, a single <a href="/wiki/Instance_(computer_science)" title="Instance (computer science)">instance</a> rather than being defined on the class). Though Ruby does not support <a href="/wiki/Multiple_inheritance" title="Multiple inheritance">multiple inheritance</a>, classes can import <a href="/wiki/Module_(programming)" class="mw-redirect" title="Module (programming)">modules</a> as mixins.</p>/n<p>Ruby has been described as a <a href="/wiki/Multi-paradigm_programming_language" class="mw-redirect" title="Multi-paradigm programming language">multi-paradigm programming language</a>: it allows procedural programming (defining functions/variables outside classes makes them part of the root, 'self' Object), with object orientation (everything is an object) or <a href="/wiki/Functional_programming" title="Functional programming">functional programming</a> (it has <a href="/wiki/Anonymous_function" title="Anonymous function">anonymous functions</a>, <a href="/wiki/Closure_(computer_science)" class="mw-redirect" title="Closure (computer science)">closures</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Continuation" title="Continuation">continuations</a>; statements all have values, and functions return the last evaluation). It has support for <a href="/wiki/Introspection_(computer_science)" class="mw-redirect" title="Introspection (computer science)">introspection</a>, <a href="/wiki/Reflection_(computer_science)" class="mw-redirect" title="Reflection (computer science)">reflection</a> and <a href="/wiki/Metaprogramming" title="Metaprogramming">metaprogramming</a>, as well as support for interpreter-based<sup id="cite_ref-87" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-87">[87]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Thread_(computer_science)" class="mw-redirect" title="Thread (computer science)">threads</a>. Ruby features <a href="/wiki/Type_system" title="Type system">dynamic typing</a>, and supports <a href="/wiki/Parametric_polymorphism" title="Parametric polymorphism">parametric polymorphism</a>.</p>/n<p>According to the Ruby <a href="/wiki/FAQ" title="FAQ">FAQ</a>, the syntax is similar to <a href="/wiki/Perl" title="Perl">Perl</a> and the semantics are similar to <a href="/wiki/Smalltalk" title="Smalltalk">Smalltalk</a> but it differs greatly from <a href="/wiki/Python_(programming_language)" title="Python (programming language)">Python</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-88" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-88">[88]</a></sup></p>/n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Syntax">Syntax</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Ruby_(programming_language)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: Syntax">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>/n<p>The syntax of Ruby is broadly similar to that of <a href="/wiki/Perl" title="Perl">Perl</a> and <a href="/wiki/Python_(programming_language)" title="Python (programming language)">Python</a>. Class and method definitions are signaled by keywords, whereas code blocks can be both defined by keywords or braces. In contrast to Perl, variables are not obligatorily prefixed with a <a href="/wiki/Sigil_(computer_programming)" title="Sigil (computer programming)">sigil</a>. When used, the sigil changes the semantics of scope of the variable. For practical purposes there is no distinction between <a href="/wiki/Expression_(programming)" class="mw-redirect" title="Expression (programming)">expressions</a> and <a href="/wiki/Statement_(programming)" class="mw-redirect" title="Statement (programming)">statements</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-89" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-89">[89]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-90" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-90">[90]</a></sup> Line breaks are significant and taken as the end of a statement; a semicolon may be equivalently used. Unlike Python, indentation is not significant.</p>/n<p>One of the differences of Ruby compared to <a href="/wiki/Python_(programming_language)" title="Python (programming language)">Python</a> and <a href="/wiki/Perl" title="Perl">Perl</a> is that Ruby keeps all of its instance variables completely private to the class and only exposes them through accessor methods (<code>attr_writer</code>, <code>attr_reader</code>, etc.). Unlike the "getter" and "setter" methods of other languages like <a href="/wiki/C%2B%2B" title="C++">C++</a> or <a href="/wiki/Java_(programming_language)" title="Java (programming language)">Java</a>, accessor methods in Ruby can be created with a single line of code via <a href="/wiki/Metaprogramming" title="Metaprogramming">metaprogramming</a>; however, accessor methods can also be created in the traditional fashion of <a href="/wiki/C%2B%2B" title="C++">C++</a> and <a href="/wiki/Java_(programming_language)" title="Java (programming language)">Java</a>. As invocation of these methods does not require the use of parentheses, it is trivial to change an instance variable into a full function, without modifying a single line of calling code or having to do any refactoring achieving similar functionality to <a href="/wiki/C_Sharp_(programming_language)" title="C Sharp (programming language)">C#</a> and <a href="/wiki/VB.NET" class="mw-redirect" title="VB.NET">VB.NET</a> property members.</p>/n<p>Python's property descriptors are similar, but come with a tradeoff in the development process. If one begins in Python by using a publicly exposed instance variable, and later changes the implementation to use a private instance variable exposed through a property descriptor, code internal to the class may need to be adjusted to use the private variable rather than the public property. Ruby’s design forces all instance variables to be private, but also provides a simple way to declare <code>set</code> and <code>get</code> methods. This is in keeping with the idea that in Ruby, one never directly accesses the internal members of a class from outside the class; rather, one passes a message to the class and receives a response.</p>/n<p>See the <a href="#Examples">Examples</a> section below for samples of code demonstrating Ruby syntax.</p>/n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Interaction">Interaction</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Ruby_(programming_language)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=18" title="Edit section: Interaction">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>/n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">See also: <a href="/wiki/Interactive_Ruby_Shell" title="Interactive Ruby Shell">Interactive Ruby Shell</a></div>/n<p>The Ruby official distribution also includes <code>irb</code>, an interactive command-line interpreter that can be used to test code quickly. The following code fragment represents a sample session using <code>irb</code>:</p>/n<div class="mw-highlight mw-content-ltr" dir="ltr">/n<pre>/n<span class="go">$ irb</span>/n<span class="gp">irb(main):001:0&gt; </span><span class="nb">puts</span> <span class="s1">'Hello, World'</span>/n<span class="go">Hello, World</span>/n<span class="go"> =&gt; nil</span>/n<span class="gp">irb(main):002:0&gt; </span><span class="mi">1</span><span class="o">+</span><span class="mi">2</span>/n<span class="go"> =&gt; 3</span>/n</pre></div>/n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Examples">Examples</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Ruby_(programming_language)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=19" title="Edit section: Examples">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>/n<table class="plainlinks metadata ambox ambox-content ambox-Unreferenced" role="presentation">/n<tr>/n<td class="mbox-image">/n<div style="width:52px"><a href="/wiki/File:Question_book-new.svg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/9/99/Question_book-new.svg/50px-Question_book-new.svg.png" width="50" height="39" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/9/99/Question_book-new.svg/75px-Question_book-new.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/9/99/Question_book-new.svg/100px-Question_book-new.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="512" data-file-height="399" /></a></div>/n</td>/n<td class="mbox-text">/n<div class="mbox-text-span">This section <b>does not <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citing_sources" title="Wikipedia:Citing sources">cite</a> any <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Verifiability" title="Wikipedia:Verifiability">sources</a></b>. <span class="hide-when-compact">Please help <a class="external text" href="//en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Ruby_(programming_language)&amp;action=edit">improve this section</a> by <a href="/wiki/Help:Introduction_to_referencing_with_Wiki_Markup/1" title="Help:Introduction to referencing with Wiki Markup/1">adding citations to reliable sources</a>. <span class="s2">"It's odd"</span>/n<span class="k">end</span>/n</pre></div>/n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Blocks_and_iterators">Blocks and iterators</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>/n<p>The two syntaxes for creating a code block:</p>/n<div class="mw-highlight mw-content-ltr" dir="ltr">/n<pre>/n<span class="p">{</span> <span Many built-in methods have such arguments:</p>/n<div class="mw-highlight mw-content-ltr" dir="ltr">/n<pre>/n<span class="no">File</span><span class="o">.</span><span class="n">open</span><span class="k">def</span> <span class="nf">remember</span><span class="p">(</span><span class="o">&amp;</span><span class="n">a_block</span><span class="p">)</span>/n  <span class="vi">@block</span> <span class="n">remember</span> <span class="p">{</span><span class="o">|</span><span class="nb">name</span><span class="o">|</span> <span class="nb">puts</span> <span class="s2">"Hello, </span><span class="si">#{</span><span class="nb">name</span><span class="si">}</span><span class="s2">!"</span><span class="p">}</span>/n/n<span class="c1"># Call the closure (note that this happens not to close over any free variables):</span>/n<span class="vi">@block</span><span class="o">.</span><span class="n">call</span><span class="p">(</span><span class="s1">'Jon'</span><span class="p">)</span>   <span class="c1"># =&gt; "Hello, Jon!"</span>/n</pre></div>/n<p>Creating an <a href="/wiki/Anonymous_function" title="Anonymous function">anonymous function</a>:</p>/n<div class="mw-highlight mw-content-ltr" dir="ltr">/n<pre>/n<span class="nb">proc</span> <span class="p">{</span><span class="o">|</span><span class="n">arg</span><span class="o">|</span> <span class="nb">puts</span> <span class="n">arg</span><span class="p">}</span>/n<span class="no">Proc</span><span class="o">.</span><span class="n">new</span> <span class="p">{</span><span class="o">|</span><span class="n">arg</span><span class="o">|</span> <span class="nb">puts</span> <span class="n">arg</span><span class="p">}</span>/n<span class="nb">lambda</span> <span class="p">{</span><span class="o">|</span><span class="n">arg</span><span class="o">|</span> <span class="nb">puts</span> <span class="n">arg</span><span class="p">}</span>/n<span class="o">-&gt;</span><span class="p">(</span><span class="n">arg</span><span class="p">)</span> <span class="p">{</span><span class="nb">puts</span> <span class="n">arg</span><span class="p">}</span>         <span class="c1"># introduced in Ruby 1.9</span>/n</pre></div>/n<p>Returning <a href="/wiki/Closure_(computer_science)" class="mw-redirect" title="Closure (computer science)">closures</a> from a method:</p>/n<div class="mw-highlight mw-content-ltr" dir="ltr">/n<pre>/n<span class="k">def</span> <span class="nf">create_set_and_get</span><span class="p">(</span><span class="n">initial_value</span><span class="o">=</span><span class="mi">0</span><span class="p">)</span> <span class="c1"># note the default value of 0</span>/n  <span class="n">closure_value</span> <span class="o">=</span> <span class="n">initial_value</span>/n  <span class="o">[</span> <span class="no">Proc</span><span class="o">.</span><span class="n">new</span> <span class="p">{</span><span class="o">|</span><span
